RATING: 5
Sweet spot is 3 players, with 2 players coming in behind that. Above 3, everyone has to be super judicious for their turns or it slogs painfully slow. Standard mid-2000s Alea components (read: cheap), and the iconography is *really tough* to make out in anything other than bright light (especially the market/festivals with those tiny numbers/colors). I generally enjoy playing (mostly because it's a novel combination of erratic turn order and tile laying), but something about it makes me go "oh, yeah... we can do that..." and as such, I never really suggest it. Lots of folks talk about the manipulation of the market as being an avenue of interest, but for some reason it's not a function that I find appealing in this game and I'm not sure why.

RATING: 8
This is a sophisticated, subtle, beautifully designed game-mechanic, wrapped up in an aesthetically rather unappealing package. Wooden resource tokens help a lot. It feels like a great multi-player solitaire puzzle. It doesn't outstay its welcome, although downtime can be a problem when a player chooses to take many turns in a row. The rules allow this but the player is heavily punished at game-end if they do this too frequently. 

There are many routes to victory so interesting strategies abound. The turn system is particularly interesting and enjoyable.

It makes a great solo game (using dummy players) - rules are discussed in the "variants" forum.

A really strong little Euro-game I think.

RATING: 6
1 play / 4 players
German edition.

LIKE: Simple engine building with tile laying. Fun to see which tiles come out next and trying to figure out combos. Many ways to score. Great turn order mechanism.
DISLIKE: Very fiddly, very dry, cube pusher. Knowing the different tiles in advance help players. Iconography is small. The game is starting to show it's age.

OVERALL: Meh. I should play this again and with more engaged players. I could feel a little tension when trying to block other players, and weighing different decisions. Unfortunately, hard to get to the table for the above reasons. (German version wasn't an issue.)

2013-01-24
8->9
Because I constantly want to play Glen More, it really deserves a 9. Along with Notre Dame, this game has become my pick for an euro to fill a 30-40min slot.

2016-04-19
9->10
The fact that Glen More has been stood the test of time for five years and it still finds its way onto the table on a regular basis has earned the game a ten in my book. The game is almost free of negatives.

First off, I really like how many tough decisions you have to make. Every move is a trade off between two or more things, since choosing to go through a door makes others close for you. And this is really the thing I enjoy the most in boardgames. You can execute multiple different strategies, although one has to be flexible since there is enough randomness and chaos to keep things fresh. However, this fluctuation does not dominate the game and players have a good sense of control. Competition over tiles and money is fierce adding a thick layer of indirect player interaction.

There are only two little minor details that I can criticise. The first one is the game end. Since you know the number of tiles left exactly, the last round is not as exciting as the others. Basically you are only min-maxing the last victory points without the decision whether to invest on future potential or cashing out. The second point of criticism is on the visual appearance. It's functional but not as exciting as it could be. However, I really have to say that the aforementioned two aspects are really really minor and do not preclude me from rating the game a ten. There is plenty to ponder on the earlier rounds, and the game is not exactly an eyesore.

RATING: 8
This is the game I try to get Carcassonne players to play.  A wonderful little strategy game that uses tile placements and different scoring tracks.  The clever bit is that scoring is relative to other players, so there isn't a single winning strategy.  Players have to watch what others are doing and adjust their strategy as they go along.

EDIT: Suburbia is surprisingly similar to this.

RATING: 7
A fast placed tile laying game, a perfect filler. As the ruler of a Scottish Clan you have to decide which tile to take from a row of tiles, taking into the account the fartest tile you take you'll give advantages to the opponents as the round is always begun by the player in last position. So if you are to much ahead then you'll probably will be the last to choose from the leftovers. After you take a tile (field, brewery, castle), you will build it into your tableaux and activate it, giving you resources, bonuses, victory points. The most valuable resource is of course whisky. The catch at the end is that if you have more tiles than the others you'll be penalized, so you will always have to consider balancing. It is fast paced and fun, with minimum of downtime.

RATING: 6
2-5(4)

The others were more enthusiastic about this one than I was. Some have called the spacial element sort of frivolous (as in, it'll punish you if you're not paying attention to your clan member placement, but otherwise it's just annoying) and I can see that, but people do get dinged on where their meeples are. Also, it will reward players who make sure to set up for maximal activation of their juiciest tiles. I do find it annoying, but I figure others think that this is a key strategic element.

The tile selection is nice, and sort of self-balancing. People skip to the better tiles and then somebody else can "clean up" the worse tiles (at their risk, of course). My issue, though, is that so many tiles feel the same. They give you a cube of a certain color, or turn a cube of a certain color to points or whatever. I don't need a deep theme or something, but I really don't want to play something that's so purely cube-pushing. Blech, maybe it's just because the tiles are so drab.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Glen More is one of those unusual games that assembles familiar mechanics in a way that seems fresh. In this case it's Alhambra-esque tile placing, Thebes-esque turn order with the player who has taken the least time playing next, and you-name-the-efficiency-game churning of resources into vps. An interesting wrinkle is in the final scoring, which rewards the frugal Scot who has built his resource churning engine with the fewest tiles and penalises the tile-happy builder who has most despoiled the Highlands.

On paper, Glen More does not look at all my kind of game, but I've enjoyed my plays of it. My one grumble concerns the thinness of the tiles and the tiny text and icons on them, which is the same in both Alea and Rio Grande editions.

RATING: 9
Absolutely lovely elegant mechanics. I was at first uncertain how much of the game was strategic, how much tactical, and how much luck, but the more I play it, the more I'm starting to see that by focusing on both the strategic and the tactical aspects, you can really make your own luck. The deeper game is in large part about jockeying for position, and getting the most benefit from what your opponents will ignore.

The game reminds me in some ways of Cuba: There's difficult decisions to make on where to place clansmen to allow most opportunity for both scoring and building in subsequent turns - but luckily it's not punishing you _quite_ so badly for making a wrong decision.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
On the surface, this game doesn't impress. The components are thin and the iconography very small... to the point it distracts away from the gameplay. But if you are able to move pass that, there is something charming and satisfying about this game. Mechanically, this charm is achieved in multiples way.

1. The rondel mechanism allows players to plan ahead while creating situations where players may be able to have several turns consecutively. But beware... taking too many tiles will lead to a strong end-game penalty. 

2. The marketplace can serve as an easy-source of coins/resources if timed right... but can also be manipulated to cut-off other players from resources they can't produce. 

3. The variable scoring is dependent on what other players have. Sometimes it is worthwhile to do something for the sole purpose of denying another player VP. 

4. There really seems to be multiple paths of victory and viable strategies. The best choice is dependent on when the tiles come out and what your opponents do.

5. The inclusion of the die, for 2/3-players, speeds up the game without adding much luck. In the end, you feel like the best player won.

RATING: 7.5
A bit of Carcassonne tile-laying, with a little Thebes in terms of how the drafting works.  It's even got a simple little market in to boot.

I really love the idea behind a game where you get to build your own little section of the board, and that each piece you build can connect with the others to create interesting combonations.

I also really like the (slightly forced) balancing mechanism where players who take a lot of tiles, run the risk of being punished over those who are a bit more selective.

RATING: 8
First Play: Rules are quite straightforward but with some important elements that must be emphasised pre-start. Once begun, the game is quite intuitive and then reveals itself as something that needs a fair degree of forethought and planning. A weather eye must be kept open for what your opponants are up to and the possibility to stifle other players plans is always a good thing.

There seem to be killer combos with the cards that need to be appreciated and blocked to stop players taking advantage.

Second game and the importance of pre-empting the game end is crucial. 

A very impressive game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
[b](just read the bold parts for my short take of this game)[/b]
I would rather do almost anything, than ever, ever, ever play a 5-player game of this flawed game, ever again.

I still probably wouldn't play it with less than 5, because I would be wasting my time on a game that really isn't worth the repeat plays relative to what else is out there that really earns my respect as a repeat play game.

1.) It has phased tiles.  That, by itself, is only mildly annoying and I don't normally fault a game for phased tiling if it's not done too over-the-top with people waiting for phase x because they know a special building is going to be there that they want.  I hate that, because the only way a new person would know that building is coming, is if they had played the game.  Homesteaders does this, and it's not too bad because normally it's only phased to keep things interesting within an engine.  Pelepones does it, but it's not too terrible, either.  Le Havre does it, but I really like it there because it's super gradual.

[b]Stacked with the random various 'tam' cards with random castles and special happy yippie-skippie win-me-the-game cards, I want to slap Matthias upside the head for requiring everyone to know what every card does before they play the game.[/b]  "Oh, there's a card that gives 2 points for every village?  MAN, that would have been nice to know at the start of the game."

[b]Yes, you can read what the cards do, but you still shouldn't be required to study a game in depth before you play it to have a chance at it. Even if you do study all the cards, until you play the game a time or two, you really don't know the implications of some of the special powers on the cards.[/b]

[b]2.) With 5 players.[/b]  Random buildings coming out every turn.  This is going to bring-out the analysis paralysis in everyone, and with 5 players, there's a guaranteed person at the table that when it gets to their turn, they're going to act like they just looked at the board 5 seconds ago and suddenly everything has changed.  

So you're looking at ~5 minutes a 'round'.  

Unless...

This has a mechanism where you select what you want by jumping ahead, and you can only go again when everyone else has caught-up and jumped ahead of you.  So, say you see something you really want?  How bad do you want it?  Do you want to wait 5 minutes before you take a turn again, or 10 minutes?  Yes.  Really.  

So eventually I catch myself going, "I do really want that tile, but I also enjoy actually playing a game.  Lets go with the closer one."  [b]I've never been punished by boredom for my game choices in a game before.[/b]

3.) If a new tile comes out that you do want, and you don't jump on it and suffer the consequences of boredom, you can kiss that tile goodbye anyway.  It's basically being railroaded into the next three available tiles whether you want them or not.  There's 5 players, and 8 tiles to choose from.  I assure you, that choice tile you want is going bye-bye.

True to form, take a look at the 'recommendations', and most do not recommend this with 5.   

I bet with 2 and 3, it might be a passable experience, but with 5, I've never wanted to slit my wrists to make a game stop before.  However, with any number of players, it's a fundamentally-flawed game that requires you to play it 2-3 times before you can really play it, anyway.

If everyone has tried it 2-3 times knows the cards, and it was only 2-4 players, it might be a pretty kickass game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Nifty little game.

It's quite fast with little to no downtime... and I find it quite original (although I might not have played as many worker placement games as others on BGG so the originality comment is purely subjective).

I played this twice and always lost against my GF.

One thing I kinda don't like is the feel that some of the special buildings (lochs and castles) are overpowered compared to others. It's a bit like the big buildings in Puerto Rico...

A quick note for those scared about the presence of the die for the 2 and 3 players game: it's no big deal and simulates pretty well an additional player (that might or might not pick up a tile you wanted). My GF -REALLY- hates randomness and she never even thought about complaining about the die.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I may have to learn to just say no at a convention.  I'm in this habit of just kind of saying "sure I'll play whatever you feel like."  And what ends up happening is that I end up playing games like Glen More.  What makes it even worse is that the person who selected the game often is VERY familiar with the game and mops the floor with me, but that's a separate issue (I must admit it partially colored my view of this game, though.)  The biggest knock I have against Glen More is just the total and utter themelessness of the game.  I have a limit on the amount of boring themeless games I can handle and this one pushed me over the edge.

Now, to be fair the way that players run around the board snatching up tiles is cool.  And I like how this determines turn order.  Plus, strategic tile-laying is often right up my alley.  But trying to build a "machine" that can churn out victory points is just not even slightly exciting to me.  I want to fly in space, fight dragons, and other fantastical stuff.  Boardgames are a method of escapism for me.  I have no interest in struggling to grow wheat in order to brew whiskey, and raising sheep to sell wool at market.  Boring, thy name is Glen More, and I'm happy I was able to try it before I bought it.  All that said, some people will absolutely love this game, and I'll completely understand why.  Heck, I'd even play it again if it was with a group of rookies.  But it will never be one I suggest.

RATING: 8
This is, simply put, a great game. I don't tend to like heavier economic Euros, as they tend to be very AP-prone or very long. This one is neither, yet the experience is fulfilling and the decision space is large. There are multiple paths to victory - you can diversify or specialise. There is a fun and rewarding spatial element with chieftain movement and villages, and tile-laying. Despite appearances, close-out is very unlikely as there are multiple copies of each tile. And to top it all off, it has a limited but functioning economy and minimal luck.

I think this is the best Euro currently available.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
There is a lot of tough choices packed into the less than an hour play time. It hits on a lot of keys for me. I like the tile placement and the choices presented by activating other tiles around where you place. I enjoy Thebes and like the mechanic where the player in the back gets the next move. The strategies around collecting resources you will need and the market are interesting. There are some interesting things in the scoring with the separation from the leader in a category and the person in last deciding points. Also the fact that you lose points if you’ve played more tiles than others brings an increased need for efficiency into the game. There is a lot to like going on. I’ve only had two plays but the first was good and the second was even better. We’ve only played with two and it’s great at that number. The dummy third player works well. I’m a little worried with the lower number of tiles to go around in a four or five player game, but look forward to trying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A little more complicated '7 Wonders,' with the three ages/rounds of scoring moving from having mostly resources (#1) to more important buildings (#2 and #3) to maximize scoring. Difference between the two is that players can play the resource market in Glen More, rather than rely on neighbors, to gain coins or acquire resources that are not available; and players have to decide where to place the new tile to maximize production. Additionally, scoring is done in a handful of ways in both games, and in Glen More your opponents can increase your scoring in other areas by not advancing. Both games have open information in tile/card selection, but in Glen More it's more apparent, as the tiles are always open to view, where in 7 Wonders one does not know a card will come back around until the pass goes through all players. The dice 'player' for the 2 and 3 player games is the most innovative of any game including Alhambra that I have played. Just an excellent game packed into a little box and board.

RATING: 8.5
This is a much bigger game than the size of the box would suggest - there's a lot going on and some nice interconnecting mechanisms to grapple with without complex rules getting in the way.  It also has a nicely novel feel to it without necessarily being groundbreaking - indeed the 'brand new' element emphasised by the rules is, surprisingly, the turn order - which was previously seen in Thebes/Jenseits von Theben and Shipyard/Neuland (though, to be fair, if the game is a success, and it deserves to be, it will probably be played by many folk who've never heard played any of those games).  In fact the placement/activation rules feel like a fresher element - while there are similarities with other games I haven't come across anything quite the same.  My rating could easily be a point higher, though I would freely admit that part of that is down to the theming.  I'm hesitating only because the first game took longer than expected.  While my expectation is that, like many games, it will go a lot faster when replayed, the addition of an extra (new) player didn't speed things any on second play.

[b]TOTAL = 446.0/50 = 8.92[/b]

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Great, simple and elegant tile laying tableau-builer with smooth gameplay, but yet very deep strategy-wise with many paths to victory (it feels light-weight but it plays as a medium-weight game).
++ I love the variable player's order turns using the time track (We must always strive to find the right balance between having great tiles first and the number of turns/tiles that we will have in the end, also while considering the excess tiles penalty).
++ Great, brilliantly simple and very effective dummy-player mechanism (for 2 and 3 players games).
++ I love the simple but fun implementation of the variable market of the ressources.
++ Good replayability due to random tiles order on the time track (especially the order of the third and last stack that are not all used during a game) and to variable player's turn order.
++ Good value, as all Alea title (component of relatively good quality, but great price point).
++ Relatively easy to teach, fast to set-up, don't take too much table space, and fast to play.


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- I wish there were more ressource cubes, to be able to populate the market with either coins or ressources cubes, since it's really easier to explain that you either take a cube and pay the coin(s) or you take the coin(s) and put back a ressource token (like Rahdo did in his run through).
-- To make it more thematic, this game could easily used animeeples and thematic ressource tokens instead of just cubes.
-- The end of the game happens quite fast sometimes (and abruptly) and thus we sometimes have a feeling of not having done everything we wish we would have done.
-- The replayability in 2-players games can be a bit lower, although still high enough.

RATING: 7
Pretty cool middle-weight game with a bit of an engine  building feel to it.  As is typical in many Eurogames, you're producing cubes to turn into points and money here, so if you're not into that, you'll hate this.

However, I thought this was a pretty cool twist on the standard Euro-feel, and I pretty much always enjoy engine building type games.  It was also very cool to see that in our game, all the players were doing very different strategies, with the score very close in the end.  I really like that the game seemingly has a lot of routes to potential victory.

All in all, this game just has some cool mechanisms that while mostly unoriginal, come together to make a game that is quite good.  I look forward to exploring it more!

RATING: 3
I had the opportunity to play [i]Glen More[/i] for the first time at last weekend’s Unity Games convention and I found it to be the most disappointing game of the Con.  For what it’s worth, I think that Glen More might appeal to fans of [thing=27173][/thing] .  I rate [i]Vikings[/i] a [b][BGCOLOR=#CC99FF][COLOR=#CC99FF]_[/COLOR]5[COLOR=#CC99FF]_[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/b], however, and [i]Glen More[/i] seems intent on beating [i]Vikings[/i] at it’s own game.

The two biggest issues I had with Glen More are its solitary play and its funky round timing.  

The idea of the game is that players move their meeple around a circular, tile-filled track, selecting tiles and adding them to their own individual tableau.  Each time a new tile is added, that tile plus all surrounding tiles are activated (in the order of the player's choice).  For those of you who have had the chance to play [thing=65781]London[/thing], tile activation creates a fair bit of lonely downtime that is reminiscent of a player running a large city. The difference, of course, is that in Glen More this happens [i]for every player on every turn of the game[/i].  A typical turn might involve (1) staring at the board weighing options, (2) selecting a tile, (3) staring at your tableau to decide where to put it, (4) placing the tile, (5) activating an adjacent tile that produces wheat, (6) activating an adjacent tile that produces cow, (7) activating an adjacent tile that produces stone, (8) using the newly placed building to change the wheat into a whisky barrel, (9) activating an adjacent building to convert the meat into VP, (10) selling the quarry to the market for 3 coins.  

To be clear, my main complaint is not about downtime or speed of play.  My main complaint is that the game is solitairish.  The sense of isolation I had playing the game was merely exacerbated by the downtime.  While my opponents are playing, I wait, but I don't care.  They are stringing together a chain of events that I'm not paying any attention to.  Their hands fly into and out of the supply, coins enter and leave the market.  They could be cheating openly for as much as I can tell from across the table.  I probably wouldn't stop them if I could.  Rarely have I played a game where I had so little emotional investment in what the players around me were doing, yet had to wait so long for them to do it.

As for my second complaint, urns in [i]Glen More[/i] are taken on a [thing=30869]Thebes[/thing]-like system (the player in the back of the line goes next), but the game instills a penalty for taking too many tiles as a sandbagging disincentive.  The game has three scoring rounds that trigger when each of three stacks of tiles run out.  The combination of the turn order system and the round-scoring trigger means that a competitive player needs to constantly count the tiles in the stack and the spaces on the board to judge how many turns they will get before the next scoring (saying nothing about predicting how many turns their opponents might get by comparison).  Of course, being novice players, at least two of my opponents failed to count during the last round, and, because they had jumped so far ahead at a time when the round end didn't seem imminent, ended their game without realizing they had.  Of course, they didn’t realize that their game was over for about 10 more minutes (there’s that lonely downtime again).  If , like me, you are the player who is fortunate enough to be in position to take the last turn when your opponents make this blunder, you can spend even more time optimizing your tile selection to set you up for the scoring.  In fact, I won the game because I was able to use my last "turn" to take three turns in a row, rocketing me from a likely 3rd into a runaway 1st (with the help of an incredibly lucky tile draw).   Needless to say, that’s about as unsatisfying as a victory can get.

I have other, lesser gripes, as well:
- I didn't like that most of the game's tiles are about resource-management-like combos (that go with the theme), but that scoring is based on performance in three categories, two of which are primarily related to the resource-management side of the game only by being in direct tension with it.  Honestly, the resource bit was the only thing that initially seemed promising to me about the game, but, given its marginalization in Glen More, its inclusion only made me realize how much I wished I were playing a different game.
- I didn't like clansman movement, which was largely trivial and seemed to exist solely to trip up inattentive or unaware players rather than to provide any meaningful tactical maneuvering.   I enjoy a healthy spatial element to erstwhile economic games.  I don’t enjoy things like Agricola’s field adjacency rules that provide little to think about, but punish those who forget to think about them.  Glen More is pretty much that. 
- I didn't like the special buildings that provide wildly divergent special powers (my win was thanks to a last-turn special building that allowed me to activate every tile on my tableau).  As a general rule, [i]I hate special power combo building.[/i]  But, in addition to providing powers, the buildings are one of the three scoring categories, and they often provide bonuses in one of the other categories, making them appear incredibly valuable (at least to first time players). 
- I didn't like the scoring system, where all points are relative to last place.  When an opponent and I started a whiskey barrel arms race in round 1, it was kind of fun.  Then I realized that my production had absolutely no effect on him (we both finished with the maximum possible whiskey bonus), and that I could see no way to meaningfully interfere with his success in that area.  That was less fun.  
- I didn't like the minimalistic components.  The tiles are small, the money flimsy, and the cubes sparse.  At least it's a cheap game, but this is one of those things that would make me not want to own it.  
- The rulebook was horrific.  We had to refer to it often to scour for niggling rules hidden in small type, or in examples, or, in one case, in a concise summary of the rule that conflicted with the natural reading of the longer, detailed rule.  Another rule was open-ended enough that we had a relatively lengthy debate about what conduct was/wasn't allowed.

The whole experience would have been a giant drag, but was saved by the company at the table.   I played with a really great group of guys who seemed to take the lackluster session in stride. I can't see any redeeming value for me in Glen More, and it's the sort of thing that I'd likely need to be persuaded to play again, as the simple time and effort of playing far outpaces any interest it holds or entertainment it provides.  It's a [b][BGCOLOR=#FF6699][COLOR=#FF6699]_[/COLOR]3[COLOR=#FF6699]_[/COLOR][/BGCOLOR][/b] (FWIW, my highest one).

RATING: 8
One of my favorite tile laying games, and the selection process for whose turn it is is well designed.  The scoring modifiers throughout the game (as well as the balancing mechanism for those with "uge tracks of land") are what makes this game very unique.

For those who don't like "economic engine" games, I think you may still like this one, as there is an "engine" aspect to it, but there are enough variables throughout the game that it won't be the primary focus.

Opponent screwage can also be a big factor in this game, so be aware that it can cause "issues" with your friends.  Not as bad as Diplomacy, but I get yelled at by my wife on a regular basis when we play, because I always seem to be the one to take that much needed tile that she so desperately wants.

RATING: 6

RATING: 7
Glen More was more or less a researched impulse buy for me. I spotted it at a local game shop while on holiday, glossed over some reviews (a little too carelessly, I’ll admit that) and when opportunity presented itself some weeks later, purchased the box at a fair price. Now although I did know about some ‘cool’ turn order mechanism (which, as it turns out, was already known to me from other games) and some tile laying, what I didn’t know was that Glen More contains a resource growth engine. As luck would have it, the engine is fairly simple and straightforward: there are at most two levels between obtaining raw resources and gaining VPs, and in the vast majority of cases there is in fact nothing in between these two (although they do constitute separate actions). The difficulty is far more in the topological restrictions deciding which actions can be activated. This makes the engine easier to grok. Another thing which helps is that stronger actions tend to come out in the last part of the game, but at an uncertain time, so players are not easily left in the dust by more experienced gamers.

Now if you are looking for a clever resource building engine where you can do what you want whenever you want (go slow now, go fast at the end; or the other way around; or ...), and don’t need the game to introduce some breaks because your fellow players are quick on the uptake of subtleties in the engine speeds, then Glen More is not for you. Simple as that: you will find it trite and simplistic. But for the likes of me, Glen More is really not that bad, perhaps even fun. The random tile draw means that players need to remain flexible whatever their chosen path and cannot settle down in fixed unbeatably efficient paths; the fact that for three rounds a [i]relative[/i] scoring takes place is a nice way of forcing players to keep up, or to slack off once the difference becomes too big. Of course knowledge of what is in the draw stacks will help you plan better, but still the need for flexibility must not be underestimated.

Speaking against Glen More are downtime at high player counts, difficulties in assessing what tiles are good for other players (as looking at someone else's little area is not easy), and a rather solitary nature. The efficiency of your engine is distilled into a few basic parameters, so you notice each other there; and of course tile track and market are the other sources of interactivity. Don’t expect brilliant gaming patterns or on-the-spot brinkmanship or whatever: you’re going to play a significant part of the game solo. 

Since I own very few resource engine games, and dislike squeezing every bit of efficiency out of them because that is what they are all about, Glen More is for the time being sufficiently likeable for me to not be regretting my purchase. I'm putting in a low rate, but if nothing funny happens I expect the rate to climb.

[b]Update[/b] Increased the rate. This is a solid little game, at least at the lower player end.

RATING: 8
Really enjoyed my first play - can really tell that there are things you're doing wrong, as you're doing them. Keeping track of future tiles, not only what's in your opponents tableau, but where his meeples are, etc.

For example, if there's a key river tile that you want, but you think he might take it, make sure he can actually place it. Because if you make play X so that he won't steal, but he could actually steal because he couldn't afford or couldn't place, you're just screwing yourself.

Decisions like that throughout - nice balance of spacial planning and efficient resource manipulation. Enjoy this more face to face than on Yucata.

RATING: 8.4
I've started playing this on Yucata as it was suggested to me as a game I would enjoy.  And indeed it is.  Takes a little bit to wrap your head around the scoring and activation, but otherwise a delight.

In this tile-laying game, players pick up one tile per turn, then placing it adjacent to a tile with a villager.  The tile then "activates" all adjacent tiles, which can help you collect resources or earn victory points.  Resources can also be bought and sold in a limited market.  The game also uses the classic "last shall be first" model of turn order.

The key here is scoring is based on a few factors - but specifically on how you compare to all other players, which provides the interactivity.  So for example, letting one player run away with a lot of whiskey barrels is NOT a good idea for anyone but him.  Get thee to a distillery!  This game is good fun and family-friendly.  It offers just enough strategy to make me scratch my head but plays quickly.  I need a copy.

RATING: 6
I like: the spatial puzzling of your territory layout & activation (so long as people are willing to play without taking too long on "where will this tile go?"), the market system (nice method of indirect trading), and the points penalty for using more tiles than the most frugal player - it provides a nice counterpressure to hoovering up as many tiles (and thus activations) as you can manage, which in turn makes it more worthwhile to jump ahead to get the just-right tile, which in turn makes the time-track system more interesting.

I dislike: the length of the time track (feels too short - I want more lookahead), the downtime between turns with more than 3 players, the incredibly tiny icons/text, and the sometimes-unintuitive iconography. (When do goods need to be different vs. the same?) I also found the placement restrictions of (clansfolk) + (river/road) a bit irritating - either one alone would have been fine, but together crossed over the line from "interesting constraints" to "annoyance", particularly in concert with the limited lookahead on the time track.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players develop their fief in Scotland, laying tiles with villages (to attract more "workers"), agricultural land (producing grain, sheep and cows), industries (meat and whisky to gain money, stone quarries)...With these resources one can build castles giving special abilities or gain access to Lochs with special abilities. There is also a market where you can buy and sell the resources you're lacking or have too much of. To score points you have to develop as efficiently as possible, because in the end the players with more tiles than the player with the smallest amount looses points. There are also three scoring rounds in which the difference with the player scoring lowest for number of whisky barrels, number of castles/lochs, and number of clan chieftains ("workers" that don't work anymore) determines how much you score. So, one always has to keep an eye on what the other players are up to.
The player who is in last position on the tile track always gets to choose a tile (some are for free, others you have to pay for). The tile has to fit to the ones you already have (river, road, land) and can only be placed next to a tile where you have a "worker". Each time a tile is placed, the adjacent tiles are activated. Careful planning of tile placement and "worker" placement is thus necessary. But then again, you never know whether you're going to be able to get that tile you want to create a powerful combination.
Some of the special tiles (castles/lochs) seem to be overpowering, especially considering their relative cost.
But all in all, this is quite a challenging and fun game.

RATING: 7
The usual "build resource engine & get points" heart, but in this case players select and place tiles into thier own tableaux with the placement triggering tiles around it. ie, Good tileplanning is critical. An elegant mechanism ensures players who choose the better tiles get fewer turns (less tiles) so there's some nice decision tension there. However, this mechanism also leads to significant downtime if you've taken the good stuff. I usually find myself taking lots of crap because I can't stand the boredom of leaping ahead, then waiting. I also dislike that the main VPs are the usual "who has most X" with its typical problem of providing a boost to players on find themselves on a lonely path. At least the game also has a way to steer your game onto the lonely path.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's your basic multiple-paths-to-victory euro. Collect resources. Certain resources enable you acquire tiles that help you along Path A; other resources lead to tiles that help with Path B, etc. However, the tile placement restrictions lift this game above the average such euro. You want to place each tile so that the most useful previously-placed tiles are re-activated, and there's the rub. You finish every game thinking you could have built a more optimal village of tiles.

RATING: 7.2
[September 2015 - 7.2]
I really love the player-turn mechanic in Glen More and think more games should use it. It definitely takes awhile to wrap your head around the strategy/gameplay and it's better played with everyone at the same experience level. For a minimal components, the theme comes through quite well.

There are hints of Puerto Rico here, but with a nice timer to it.

The special cards are one of my few complaints. There are a couple super-powerful ones that can change the game. The other is the penalty for having more tiles than others, which to me sort of ruins the player-turn mechanic. Hanging back to get more tiles can't really be played as a full-on strategy.

Need to play more.  Tough to find in the United States.

RATING: 5
[ImageID=747865]

Glen More, by Mattias Cramer, was another surprise out of BGG.Con for me, a light game that combines both an interesting time mechanism, a zero-sum market, and an innovative tile place mechanic into a solid, broad game that allows for multiple strategic options yet rewards opportunistic risk taking.

The second thing a player does on their turn is place a tile into their area. I am going to explain it first, however, because it provides a good deal of context for the first part of a player’s turn. In order to place a tile into their area, the player needs to meet two criterion: first, the tile must be placed in such that it is adjacent to one of their clansmen meeples; second, the tile’s side features must match those on the board, with the only features of real note being a river that moves north and south, and a road that moves east and west. As the tiles are placed, the owner pays for the resource requirements of the tile either by removing those resources from their board or by buying them from the market. After the tile is placed then that tile, and all adjacent tiles are immediately activated, thus producing resources, converting resources to victory points, allowing the movement of clansmen, or whatever else that particular tile does. In general, tiles can only be activated when new tiles are placed adjacent to them, thus making the order of tile placement, and the movement of the clanspeople very, very important.

The first thing a player does on their turn is to move their meeple along the time track that circles the central board area, moving it to a tile, then taking the tile and adding it to their overall board. Then the player whose meeple is closest to the back of the time track can move their meeple to take a tile. If the same player who is at the back of the line stays at the back of the line then they are allowed to take multiple tiles in a row. This has a number of interesting implications, both because by getting more tiles you have more opportunities to activate your tile set, but also because individuals lose victory points at the end of the game based on the relative size of their tile set, with larger numbers of tiles causing a bigger victory point loss.

Victory points are awarded based on resource conversion tiles that are acquired during play, as well as relative majorities of whiskey barrels, chieftains (retired clanspeople), and bonus cards (acquired by taking castle or loch tiles). Points for majorities are scored three times during the game, with a relative amount of parity available between all of the different scoring options; it seems quite possible to combine victory point generation in all sorts of ways and still pull of a victory.

I found Glen More to be fairly clever and entertaining at first but further exploration showed it to be just as shallow as most lighter games. Helvetia (also by the same designer), had a similar lack of depth so I may avoid him in the future. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.6

It is quite enjoyable and I would like to see this mechanism used in other games. Overall Glen more is a wonderful small but deep euro.

RATING: 6
Players are Scottish lairds in the 16th century, trying to expand and work their land holdings.  Mechanically simple game, where the player trailing on the action track takes the next turn (think Jenseit von Theben's turn order mechanic), advancing their pawn as far as they like and claiming a land tile to add to their holding.  The placed tile activates for some sort of ability, as well as every adjacent tile, setting up a nice puzzle of where to position tiles for maximum benefit.  Added restrictions, related to your meeples, add to the decision tree.  Tiles typically produce stuff, move pieces or mince stuff into victory points.  Three scoring rounds award extra points for having chiefs, special holdings and whiskey barrels.  So we've seen it all before, but rarely in a nice tight package with simple, intuitive rules that asks some nice questions of the players.  Enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I myself like the scoring mechanics. The way it works is rather unique. Player try to score in three different categories (number of whiskey barrels, number of chieftains and tam hats, and finally number of special buildings). They sum up the total in all 3 different categories respectively and compare them with the player who has the least in each category.

With the difference you get, you refer to the scoring table and get that many points.

Also, at the end game, players with more tiles than the players with the least, are required to deduct 3 VPs for each differences. This I think is good to not promote the runaway leader, and to encourage players with less VPs to do some catch-up

Another thing to admire, is the turn order mechanics, People say that it is somehow a little similar to Thebes. If you choose to jump too far, especially to grab tiles that you want the most, your next turn will be late. 

BUT! I have only one gripe, and it can be huge for a person like me. The components! Come on Alea, you can do better.

RATING: 6
[2 plays] I think a common problem with a lot of current games, or with the teaching of those games, is that because the game involves a lot of resource conversion, what you spend most of the game doing doesn't really directly help you at all in winning the game. Thus it's easy to have your attention engaged by the main activity and only notice when it's too late that you haven't actually done the thing that counts for victory. 

So if you play a few times, you get the hang of it and can play more interesting and strategic games.  If you just play once and move on, you have a string of weirdly frustrating experiences.

All that notwithstanding, Glen More has its merits. I like the way the scoring works: instead of the typical game where you're maybe thinking it's hopeless to catch up with the leader, in this game you can be the worst player in the game, and if you do something to help yourself, it directly drags down everyone else.  That's pretty good.

RATING: 5
I love the element of time in games a la Thebes and Tinners Trail, so it was with great surprise that this game did not appeal to me. I've only played a couple of times, but I found the game lacked a certain something. I found myself not caring about building my "city" nor what the other players were doing. The spatial element of the tiles seemed more annoying to me than fun, and in any case, it slowed everyone down as they wanted to make sure they didn't screw themselves with a bad placement.

I got this cheaply at Essen 2011 and I was pleased with the purchase for the price. Still, it was fairly good for what it offers, and simpler in result than the very long rules presaged. The game is nicely structured by design, but it's another tight MPS and they don't spell FUN for me.

RATING: 6.5
An interesting and unique take on the tile laying genre, with a basic economic focus, Glen More is a moderately entertaining and relatively accessible game for two to five players.  I have yet to play a game with more than three so far, and it seems that four players is the sweet spot, but I've still found the game to be generally a good time, with a moderate amount of depth to it.  The player is given a fair amount of choice in how they develop their clan's economy, and I really enjoy the trade-off in being able to jump ahead of the competition to grab a really desirable tile, but then having to suffer the consequences of losing a few turns while the other players catch up on the track; I also like the related concept that you are rewarded at the end for being "efficient" and not overdeveloping your land by building too many tiles.

The game seems to go by rather slow however, despite rarely lasting more than one hour.  There's also no player interaction, and I've never felt particularly excited or invested in the game, which makes winning or losing far less engaging.  Not a personal favorite of mine, and I feel it has kind of a limited shelf life (though, perhaps a few four or five player games will change my mind), but I can't say I haven't enjoyed the game thus far.

RATING: 8
This game exceeded my expectations, offering some interesting bits I've seen in other games in one fairly elegant, albeit themeless, package.  The "time as a resource" mechanism from games like Thebes, the spatial tile placement, the goods collection, and the many different ways to collect points all combine to form an engaging experience that feels somewhat unique.  The decision of which tile to collect each turn can be agonizing in and of itself.  I really like the way the meeples on the board can be used for two conflicting purposes: keeping options open for where to place future tiles, and being moved off the board to score extra points as chieftans.

RATING: 6
Essentially an optimization game, where the limited interaction comes from others snatching tiles before you. Moreover, the luck of the draw can be important as it's not a bad move to jump ahead quite a bit to grab a powerful castle.

Talking about castles, these appeared unbalanced to me as they give you two scoring resources: chieftains and special cards.

All that said, there are a few things I like about the game and I'd gladly give it another go, which may change my rating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In Glen More, players each build a landscape from tiles collected from a common pool. Those tiles kind of construct an economic engine but much of the effect usually associated with engine-building games is mitigated by how the tile-laying works. You cannot build wherever you want and to actually use previously built tiles you need to activate them by placing new tiles next to them. That makes it quite a bit more forgiving than other entries in that category.

While Glen More is thematically a game about whisky, that is only one of the avenues that can be pursued. Several paths to victory and some neat mechanics (not all of them new or unique but nicely executed) make it a pleasant game. That the only means of interaction is to snatch away tiles other players might need is a bit of a letdown, however.

RATING: 7
 Never heard of this game before. Didn't really get sucked in by the theme (resource management in Scotland, 1300s). Gameplay started off a little slow and simple, and I didn't really get the appeal. However, Glen More really picked up by the mid- and endgame. The resource track and warehouse mechanic is fresh and a very neat way to manage player interests and balance resource acquisition. The rules governing territory building were also solid and it was enjoyable to figure out ways to become efficient. I was happy to see some new ways of playing a resource management tile game - a genre that has been thoroughly worked - but the designer, Cramer, got a bit more sweet juice out of it! Unfortunately, the theme feels sooo tacked on (even more so than your standard Knizia) that it makes this game feel relatively abstract.

RATING: 8
I played this game years ago and really liked it and have only recently played it again since getting into modern boardgaming and man did I enjoy it.

I love the way the tile selection is done with the last person on the track being the one to select. There are various resources and the way you place tiles on your board and procing them is so good.

The pacing in this game is also excellent with multiple scoring rounds and more powerful tiles coming out later. Even the way they balance the number of tiles you have at the end of the game punishes you so that during the game, you aren't as punished for jumping ahead for a good tile.

The overall design of this game is so great with many aspects coming together to create a tight game with lots of interesting decisions.

RATING: 8
Awesome!  We've played 3 times and I'm already hooked.  :)

UPDATE: My wife isn't thrilled with the theme.  It doesn't "feel" or look like she's building a network of villages in the same way that Carcassonne looks like you're creating the map of the landscape, or in the same sense that in Agricola you can look at your farm when you're done and imagine yourself living there.  I still really like the game, but am inclined to agree with her on this point.  Multi-layer strategy: Check.  Thematic implementation: Not as much as it could have been, perhaps.

UPDATE: We fell out of love with the lack of theme, I think.  It has deeper strategy than Carcassonne, but at least Carcassonne looks like the map of your cities and farms and roads.  For some reason we find that to be more satisfying despite the simplicity of that game.  So we decided to sell Glen More and make room for other games.

RATING: 6
I enjoyed my first play of Glen More, but it crashed hard with further play. It's hard not to compare Glen More to the similar Vikings, which is a much better game.

The most interesting decisions are where to leave your clansmen, and the way that this limits your selection of tiles in future turns. But slowly building up those margins of chieftains and barrels just feels . . . boring. Games this solitaire-ish need to present players with a more interesting puzzle to solve and/or more ability to plan going forward.

I really, really, really dislike Glen More's cheap look and its muted, clashing color palette. Alea also skimped on the components. There aren't enough resources and VP counters in the box.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.1
Another very good Alea title. At first glance there are a lot of possible strategies. The one minor quibble I have is the 3 player game, where a die is used to randomly remove tiles from the game (simulating a 4th player). 

Unlike another player, where you (sometimes) can predict what will be taken (or even try to 'force' a tile upon them), the die just removes one of the first 3 tiles (50% for the first, 33% for the second and 17% for the third).This mechanism can result in 'illogical' selection and sometimes makes it a bit harder for laonger term planning.

The very limited market is a brutal mechanism, and has got a huge potential to screw other players (or oneself) - I like it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think this game is relatively easy to teach, but takes some time to master.  There are lots of choices to make, because there are many tradeoffs -- coins, goods, whisky, cards, etc.  The geographical element adds an additional challenge (because a new tile placement depends on where your clansmen are, and activates the adjacent tiles to the newly-placed one).  

The turn order mechanism is novel and interesting.  Knowing when to jump far ahead on the track and when to go short distances is key.  It's tempting to pick up a lot of tiles when the players are way ahead on the track, but each tile is effectively like spending 3 points, so it's important to be efficient.  Also, when playing with 2 or 3, the extra player's die roll comes into play, which causes some tiles to disappear.  That can throw off your strategy if you're planning on picking up a particular tile which ends up being discarded by the extra player.

Early in the game, cattle (brown cubes) are not useful, but grey and green cubes are very useful for building certain tiles.  Yellow cubes (wheat) are useful for distilling into whisky, which are good for points and are also required to build the point-generating tavern tiles in the #3 stack of tiles.

The markets are surprisingly good tiles.  The one that gives you 12 points for selling 5 different goods cubes is especially good, and can be a game-winner if you can activate it more than once.  It does take a lot of goods, but you might be able to buy those goods from the board for 1 coin each, which is totally worth it.

It's important to pay attention to when the game is about to end, because if you have no more turns left, you want to sell all your goods for coins (each coin is worth a point, and selling your goods prevents others from selling to the bank for those coins) and to move all your clansmen off the board to become chieftains (to compete in the final chieftain scoring).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Nice little game, with more going on than is immediately visible.

Tactical tile playing, and planning for the next tile(s), timing of claims/purchases/sales/turns, action winning, point grabbing or scoring accumulation prior to scoring rounds- a lot to think about, and it keeps changing with the next tiles revealed by other players.

So there's some 'grab it while you can,' but if you miss an opportunity, or fail to plan you could miss out on a critical action you find you need- and it's very easy to do that by mistake.

It went a little long for what it is, but this was also a first play, so I spent more time in the rules than I expect to in the future.

An attractive little game, which could have more thematic art, but what it has is very nice, and is not overdone.

I'm starting this at a 7- I think I will always want to play, but I don't think I'd turn down a game either. A good addition to the Alea Mid-sized box series.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Rating based on around 7 plays. This is a tile-laying game, similar to Carcasonne, but with everybody creating their own little network of villages, resources, and production capability, such that you could call this a "civ-lite" kind of game. Points are scored in three categories: whiskey, special places, and chieftains/tams. VP is a sliding, non-linear scale, based on the delta between your number of items against the player with the least. Players select tiles from a novel rondel-like arrangement, with turn order based on whomever happens to be on the tail end. The selected tile is then positioned relative to the other tiles of the player's tableau based on some placement rules; all surrounding tiles and the tile itself is activated to produce resources or VP.

It is light enough to play quickly, but the varied mechanisms are a bit difficult to grasp at first, and it will take time to explain. There is enough tactical and strategic play to keep the interest of novices and veterans, that it should see more play. There is a lot of gaming goodness in that tiny little box. However, after the initial exposure, I am not too excited to play it again so soon (I still can't articulate why), and hence my initial '7'. It may improve.

Update (03/02/12) 8 plays: I wasn't too excited to play this after being away from it for five months. It is an interesting game, but my initial enthusiasm for the game has faded considerably. Again, I can't articulate why. Holding steady at a '7', but I see this game having a short half-life.

Update (04/21/12) 9 plays: This is like each player playing their own version of Carcasonne: instead of picking off the top of a deck, players have choices from a rectangular arrangement on which tile to pick; players then take that tile and add to their own collection, instead of adding to a collective. The latest play was somewhat exciting, as a new strategy was uncovered.

Update (06/13/2015) 10 plays: Some people had expressed an interest, so we broke it out. It soon became apparent why it was collecting dust on the shelf for the past three years: fiddly components, fiddly rules, and too much downtime, all that has been better implemented as a computer game. General complaints that components needed to be bigger, with text being easier to read. It was like what happened with La Isla, where the publisher took everything and shrank it down to fit into a small box--bad move. It is a good design, with an innovative tile-selection/turn-order mechanism, but it reminded us of the fiddliness of Suburbia and Castles of Mad King Ludwig, where players took too much time to decide where to place a tile on their expanding village tableau. That was the initial major gripe of CoMKL--too much downtime. I think we've been recently spoiled with sleeker, smoother games like Deus and Ginkgopolis that play quickly in about an hour. With five players, and having to rehash the rules, this exercise took over two hours, where the expectation was it was supposed to be a quick, simple game of an hour. Just like the other games, this requires frequent play to familiarize oneself in order to play quickly. It's not bad, but it's not great, so it doesn't come out as often. Infrequent play means forgotten rules and an generally unsatisfying, "meh", gaming experience. Dropping to a "6". 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
My estimation of this game has gone up from my initial play. I think it is one of the better "short but not filler" games I've played. That is, games that are less than ~60 minutes but have more strategy/planning/tactics than you expect from most "fillers".

I think the spatial aspect of the tile laying is what pushes it over the top for me. It combines everything into a very "efficiency engine" that, for some reason, isn't as dry as, say, St. Petersburg.

I have some concern that some of the special buildings are too overwhelming in scoring (and too subtle for newbies to understand their worth) but I dunno if I'll ever play this enough for that to be a real problem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.1
You build a personal tableau of tiles that generate resources or converts them into points. There is a spacial aspect in that you can only build around where you have a guy standing. Tiles with a river always flows vertial, the road horizontal, and some tiles have no special attributes so they can be placed more freely.

A few neat things about the game:
Scoring is literally relative to what everyone else is doing, so it's got that same-strategy-won't-work every time thing going for it. Player order is determined by the person standing furthest in the back, so skipping far ahead to snag the tile you want may cost you some turns (which might or might not be a bad thing). And money never enters* or leaves the game--if you need to buy a resource, the money goes on top of a spot on the center board signifying that now that one unit of that resource can be sold for that money, or the next person who buys that type of resource from the bank has to pay a higher price.

RATING: 7
If you like games like stone age and/or powergrid, you'll most likely enjoy this game. Solid game that we always come back to. Plays like a combination of Seeland and Alhambra. 


You have a track that you move around to purchase tiles(how far you go affects how many turns you can get in a game). Once you purchase a tile, you place it attached to your village, with certain placement restrictions. Where you place it, determines what resources you can gain.

Solid mechanics and balance in this game. It's a go-to staple amongst our horde of boxes.

RATING: 7.75
[b]2 Player Review: [/b]

This is the game that made it painfully obvious my talent lies in long-term planning, while Yuki's is all about the short-term goals and how to make every turn count. Because of the constantly changing nature of what tiles are available each turn, I am unable to commit to a strategy. With that in mind though I always look forward to our next match so I can learn from the master and hopefully win consistently for a few matches some day. 

The presentation of the titles is fantastic and the constriction of placement based on the road or river is clever and only makes the tiles with no such restriction more sorely sort after. The market allows for the purchasing of resources which gives a little more leeway to the game play in case you don't have the exact resources available. 

[b]Pros:[/b]

- You feel like your building your own corner of Scotland. 
- Plays quickly with lots of choices to agonize over turn. 
- It's great fun to collect whiskey barrels!

[b]Cons:[/b]

-Once you fall behind it's difficult to catch-up.
-The randomness of the dummy player leads to a lot of frustration in a 2 player game.

RATING: 9
Everything about this game is a balancing act!  How far do you move ahead to get your next tile?  How many tiles should you build?  How many chieftans should you promote?

This has lots of little clever aspects to it that appeal to me: moving around the board picking up tiles, the turn-order mechanism, the tile-laying rules, the special tiles, etc. I find it to be on the lighter end of medium-weight games, although I've experienced first-hand that it might be complicated for some people. It probably helps to play it once or twice in order to have an idea of what kinds of tiles will be available throughout the game and how you will use the various production mechanisms to score points. 

Also, the game reminded me heavily of Suburbia (of course, I know that Suburbia is newer), but I [i]much[/i] prefer this one.

RATING: 7
Most fun I've ever had playing multiplayer solitaire (and that's not a moniker that I throw around liberally). Glen More presents an elegant, interesting, and well-balanced system to tinker with. The turn track evokes fond memories of Neuland (which should make that great game easier to teach!), and the closed market is an impressively simple implementation that I've never seen before.

Biggest issues are subpar component quality (which makes the game tough to learn) and how it skews from "tactical" to "wildly tactical" once you go from 3 players to 4 or 5. Also a lot of downtime in the 5 player game, though I did not see that when playing with 4.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Rating after 1 play.  "Alhambra squared."  This is a beautiful tile-laying game (like Alhambra) but with far more strategic play.  The player track is a brilliant way to offer more choice in tiles.  Do you jump ahead for a particularly good one, effectively giving up some turns?  You're rarely shut out of a tile by sheer luck, if you want it badly enough.  And the market is a brilliant way to balance resources.  It's tough to get a balanced economic engine, since early placements get harder to activate over time, but the market gives you options as long as, again, you're willing to pay the price.  This feels like trading in other games, but with pricing  discipline.  That is, one player can't be generous out of good nature.  If you're underproducing wheat and need it for your distillery, you will have to pay higher prices as you buy more (unless of course there are two players overproducing wheat, in which case they may both be selling and keeping the price down.  I love this.).  After one play I don't have a firm grasp on strategy, but there certainly appear to be multiple paths to victory.  The players in my first game took very different paths (one with a virtual whiskey monopoly and a big lead in chieftans), but the scores were close.  Looking forward to more plays.  Packs a lot of strategic punch and thematic appeal (the mechanisms may not be thematic, but the tiles are evocative) in a small box.

RATING: 3
Played once with five.  A couple rules were interpreted incorrectly, which may have changed the outcome, but I didn't really care.

Glen More isn't reminiscent of too many games I've played, and yet, it is reminiscent of many games I have played.  It is more or less a puzzle game with resource and time management.  There are so many ways to score that it seems like pretty much everything is a viable strategy.  Whatever you do, you're going to be able to score points from it, deny points from other players, or make resources that can be used to score points.  Because having less tiles than opponents, tile/points efficiency matters too.

There's not much tension, and your decision tree is small.  The icons are horrid and miniscule.  Some of them are seriously only 2mm.  If they were four times the size, you would be able to read the tiles from a distance, but it was very hard to see what tiles did at all.

Five is too many people for this game.  The downtime is bad.  I'd be willing to give the game another shot, but probably with only 3 players max, and I wouldn't have high expectations.

2017-02-28

Initial Rating: 7.0 (February 2017)

I finally played Glen more face to face. I have previously played a couple of times online and, although I understood the core mechanism of the game, I had some holes in the rules. I always lost my games and I didn’t know why. A friend explained the game to me and it turned out the biggest you village the more points you will lose at the end :D. 

I bought the game on 2015 and I finally played it. In general I dislike tile laying games, but I enjoyed Glen More.

The rules are pretty easy to teach, you can do it under 10 minutes. The hardest are the special tiles but you can explain them as they appear. The game moves at a fast pace, playtime is around 60 minutes.

The game´s components are very flimsy, furthermore, it was a very expensive game, I had to import from Germany as it was out of print in America. The art is ok, but the tiles have tiny text on them that isn’t easy to read.

Best with 4 fast players, with more I begin to feel the downtime.

Ok theme, but it doesn’t excite me (that’s ok for an Euro game). In any case it is very thin.

The game´s decisions are very interesting (namely, which tile to take, where to put it, how to move your meeples inside your village and how far to jump ahead in the turn order track). Going last all the time seems great, but, as I wrote before, if you take a lot more tiles that your opponents, at the end of the game you will lose a lot of points, so you have to strike a balance between opportunistic turn order and village size, I love this (and that’s missing from Kraftwagen, a game that uses the same mechanism but do not penalize taking a lot of extra actions). Glen More is more deep that I initially thought.

I also enjoy the spatial element of building your village. It seems the game has many paths to victory, but I need more plays to verify this point.

Bottom line, Glen More is a good Euro game and a solid 7.0 to me. It plays very fast for a game of this kind and I want to explore it further. It is a keeper in my game collection. I know a second edition of the game is coming, but I’m not sure if I will get it, time will tell.

One of 2010 favorites.

RATING: 7
The surprise hit of fall 2010 for me, this light game wasn’t on my radar at all until a somewhat serendipitous play at BGG.CON.  The time track is the standout feature, and shares with the other games using this mechanic that it is nothing like the others.  There is a heavily spatial element which is somewhat reminiscent of Vikings.  The scoring is an interesting back-and-forth between resource conversion activities and long-term majority scoring.  I don’t think a player can get by totally avoiding the resource conversions but certainly the all-out pointmongering seems balanced with a more hybrid long-term approach, which is good.

At the Alea small box price point, this is a likely purchase.  A fair bit of game in a small package.

RATING: 5.8
I had read up on this on the Geek and was excited to try. Unfortunately I like it a lot less than I thought I would going in.

Disliked: new player extreme handicap. new players don't know the best "power combos" and tile distribution; this can also really screw up the game (by screw up the game I mean 80% of visible tiles being unavailable to anyone at all) by skipping critical resources early in the game not knowing more won't be available; constraint of using as few tiles as possible was frustrating when attempting to attend the various VP generating avenues. In the end a small dose of fun with a larger dose of frustrating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One of the best games of 2010, somehow lost in the crowd after a low profile release early in the year. 

Each player undertakes the role of a clan in medieval Scotland, pushing outward into an expanse of land that includes villages, castles, lakes (even Loch Ness), pastures, woods, fairgrounds, distilleries, and so on. This expanse is built out of tiles in a Carcassonne-like fashion, with some great twists. First, tiles are drafted from the board using the Thebes-system for time-lapse: a player who takes a tile farther in the queue will have to wait a while before getting another turn. I've always enjoyed this tradeoff in games like Thebes and Tinners' Trail, and I found it well-implemented here. Is it worth jumping way ahead for a good tile, and giving other players an extra turn or two? How badly do I want it? Second, the game includes an extremely well-tuned resource market that provides tough tradeoffs and gratifying flexibility. Essentially, the market allows players to take tiles they are otherwise not prepared for (for example, building a castle even if they have not hauled stone from a quarry), but the game's economy is closed, so any money used to purchase goods will ultimately end up in the hands of another player. Very slick. Third, players must carefully move clan-members around on their board to enable effective expansion, and each tile added to the board activates all of the tiles surrounding it. Fourth, the game also rewards players for specializing (always a good thing in my book), and also penalizes the players who took the most tiles at the end of the game, essentially rewarding the players who made the most of a slim tile-set. Add in some special-buildings with unique perks, and you've got a fabulous game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Players move around a board and select tiles many of which create resources. They need to be played orthogonal or diagonal to your meeples and connected correctly to the other tiles. To buy certain tiles requires resources. There are various ways to gain victory points such as coverting resources with certain tiles, buying special cards, selling resources on the market, removing a meeple, and making barrels of ale. Also try not to get too many more tiles than the person with the least since you will lose points.

Great game, that I'd play any day with any number of players.

RATING: 7
We had heard some good things about [thing=66362][/thing], so we were interested, particularly since it sounded like a light economic game combined with the tile laying of [thing=822][/thing].  We were able to get a copy and give it a try.

The game went over pretty well.  The turn and tile selection track is a neat idea.  The tile laying and activation mechanism is interesting---since you can only activate and use a tile that's adjacent to the one you placed, you can only use a particular tile a limited amount of time.  As a result, you do get a feeling of using up and exhausting your resources and being forced to expand into newer areas.  There do seem to be several ways to score points, as well as special buildings that can help a particular strategy.  The scoring does feel a little fiddly (it's all relative positioning  between players), but you get used to it after several plays.  

The two player game does have sort of a dummy player, but it's really just a die that randomly removes some of the tiles.  Like "Dirk" in [thing=6249][/thing], it doesn't take too much time to implement, and it gives the game a little bit of variety.  It's nothing to be worried about.  We also haven't really utilized the market too much.  On the other hand, it is a relatively light game with a lot of familiar ideas, but it does scratch that itch in a short amount of time.

RATING: 8
This game really rewards intelligent play and once I do something intelligent in the game I will update the comment below.

Glen More has aspects familiar to those who have played Carcasonne & Tutankhamun.  But through incredible design reveals how puny these games are.  Glen More is a musclebound game with real strength, real ambition and replayability.  A powerhouse in a relatively small box.

The combination of tiles, workers & resources create a variety of routes to victory, which though may be difficult to grasp at first, rewards continued play.  I lost by a distance, my best turn being the last getting several resources to high scoring markets, but Glen More is certainly a game I would like to revisit.

RATING: 7
The rondel mechanic causes you to have to adapt your strategy a bit each game based on which tiles show up when despite always using the same tiles, and I also like the tricky task of placing your tiles optimally. I wouldn't say it's the most replayable game ever, but which special tiles and resource to VP conversion tiles you can get a hand on can really change the valuation of your tiles. Also, that you can have fewer (and potentially want fewer turns to prevent the negative VPs at the end of hte game) turns depending on how far you move on the rondel is mechanic not utilized much. However, the theme is completely pasted on, and component quality is pretty bad.

One of my friends compared this game to Suburbia, as both rely heavily on tile placement, both require you to get certain tile combos to build an optimal engine, and both have a mechanism to make you pay for taking tiles that more recently appeared (money vs. # of turns). I've never thought of it that way before, but now I guess they also both share the bad similarity of using most/all of their tiles each game instead of having more variability, and yet how early each tile shows up can really affect the game.

RATING: 10
It combines engine-building, tile-laying, and drafting in a very tight package. I love the depth of this game relative to its play length (30 minutes?).

The variety of possible strategies is great. I've seen people win with all kinds of extremes, as well as balanced approaches.

This kind of track for picking actions is always very interesting. Other games have used similar approaches, but what makes Glen More stand out is that you're penalized for having lots of tiles on your board, so you're incentivized to jump ahead to the juicy tiles instead of just picking one of the 2-3 tiles in front of you.

RATING: 7
An interesting Euro where you are laying tiles and activating nearby tiles.  Selecting tiles is done where you move forward on track to draft the tiles but you can go again if you are behind all other players.  There are multiple paths to victory including having a small number of tiles or large tile numbers.  You have different types of goods you can produce including whiskey. Players lose points at the end if there fields are the largest compared to the player with smallest field.  

An excellent game with somewhat unique mechanics when it came out.

RATING: 7

SEE: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/629737/should-i-bother-glen-more

Very respectable BGG Rating (although NOT sub-categorized as a Family - read: gateway - Game). 

CONCLUSION: Worth getting BUT not necessarily immediately; considering so many unplayed games that BGG ranks as a "Family Game." NOTE: If a compelling argument could be made that this game is great FUN, then the conclusion may change. 

Follow-Up: Ryan Metzler's review makes this game seem rather enjoyable... http://boardgamegeek.com/video/13813/glen-more/dice-tower-reviews-glen-more

Another positive review (although he mentions that it is NOT necessarily a "couple's game" per se): http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/779447/a-masterpiece

A VERY compelling review (and one that suggests it could be an enjoyable "couple's game"): http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/706820/drivethruredux-4-glen-more-and-7-wonders-revisited

RATING: 7.5
Glen More feels a bit like it was released a decade earlier than it actually was. It's an archetypal Euro; dry/loose theme, small amount of luck, indirect conflict, multiple paths to victory, and lots of bits of wood. These are things that I like, providing that the gameplay is interesting (which it is).

Unfortunately, even though I really enjoy playing Glen More, I don't play it very often. This is probably because it is such an innocuous game (in its small, bland box) that it doesn't leap out at me when I'm considering what to play. I guess I'll have to try and remind myself of this game a bit more often.
