RATING: 8
Compactness is something I value a lot in game design, and Glen More was apparently designed by somebody just as enticed by efficiency. Small meeples race around a small track to snap up small tiles that are subsequently placed in your small building area so that you gain the biggest amount of small resources and no less small victory point counters. What's not to love?

Apart from that (possibly rather special) appeal , Glen More offers a lot of planning, smart use of the Jenseits von Theben time track, and all of it in a rather small (here I go again) amount of time.

Edit: introduced the game to my parents. It was very hard to get them to learn all the ins and outs of the game, but at the end scoring they actually really liked the game. I also like the puzzle-element in the game (how to place your tiles).

RATING: 8

Resource management (cube pushing)?  Check.

Variable Player Order? Check.
Economic Engine? Check.
Multiple Scoring Paths? Check.

RATING: 7.7
1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Really enjoyable tile-laying game where you constantly have the pull between taking the readily available options or making a longer jump to secure yourself that tile you desperately want, while at the same time leaving your opponent(s) with less competition for the tiles you skipped.

The mechanics are really tight, like the market capping both how much of a resource can be bought or sold, and how that becomes like a double-edged sword, as one player might gain a lot of money from selling a resource, but at the same time if they do they are opening up for opponents to buy the resource to afford certain tiles - and vice versa, a player might buy the last resource, but know they are opening up for an opponent to sell theirs for a higher price.

And although it's no secret when round scorings are triggered, so far I've experienced that the player who doesn't directly trigger it forgets to plan for it, and the triggering player can use that to their advantage to earn a couple of extra points with a well-timed tile-choice.

Finally I like that the end game scoring includes a penalty for players who decided to build big, making the game more balanced as building efficiently ultimately becomes more important than building big.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
One of the best games from 2010.

A tense and meaningful gameplay packed into a short playtime is for sure a winner in my book.

It could be descriped as a standard euro, but with the mix of allready known mechanics it manages to feel fresh, innovative and engaging. A label I unfortunately cant give a lot of euros nowadays.

There are many different strategies and ways to score VP, but after several plays everything seems very well ballanced.
What seems to matter the most is, who most effectively carries out their chosen strategy. 
Managing your tableau is key, as clever placement of tiles can provide very powerfull combos when activated.

In short, this is a great game that grows on you with each play.

RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]23-Jan-2013[/u]: I have several plays of this one, but many of them were me trying to come up with [article=15917977]my own set of rules[/article] based on [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/643104/anyone-worked-up-a-solo-variant/new]this Solo variant thread.[/url]

Anyway, I really like this game and hope to get it to the table with opponents some day.  It's got elements of Carcassonne and 7 Wonders in it...two games that I enjoy a lot.  You draft tiles from the central board and play them in your own mini-Carc landscape.  When you do, you may activate that tile and any tiles within a one-tile radius.  This gives you resources which you can convert to VP.  There is more to it, obviously, but I think it is really well put together.

I do enjoy the tile-laying mechanism and the various paths you can take to gain VP.  It's not overly thinky, but there is definitely strategy in this little game.  I really hope my family will latch onto this one.

[u]24-Jan-2014[/u]:  I finally got in a couple of multiplayer games of this one, and I still think it's a fantastic game.  Not sure how much my family enjoyed it, so I don't know if it'll see the table all that much.  But personally, I think this is a solid entry into the Eurogame realm with tile-laying, a spacial aspect to consider, and a 7 Wonders feel to the resource management / point scoring system.  With the small box and lower price tag, it's a good one to have in the collection.  Just hope I can play it more often.

RATING: 8
A resource engine builder game with many familiar mechanics yet a unique feel.  It uses the Neuland turn order mechanic where you can choose a purchase tile as far up as possible but you don't get to take your turn again until everyone else has passed you.  There are a number of different combinations one can build and each player must manage their actions by placing bought tiles on a personal map which triggers adjacent tiles.  Later in the game resources can be used to make other resources (whiskey for example).  I haven't seen any dominant strategy yet.

RATING: 8.5
Played once with 5 people.  It was the first game for everyone I played with, and the scores were incredibly tight (36 - 46, with four of us between 40 and 45).  

This game is filled with very neat mechanics.  Players choose a tile to place on their map; they can only place tiles near where they have meeples, and only tiles adjacent to the one they just placed activate.  So, spatially arranging your tiles in an optimal order is absolutely essential.  

Also, the order in which you choose tiles varies greatly, and so players may end up with a different number of turns.  This is elegantly balanced out at the end-game scoring, and leads to widely different strategies being successful.

All in all a very neat, meaty game that I want to play again.

RATING: 8
I've only played this online as it seems to be out of print. I'm tempted to track down Lunarchitects (the ill-fated reskin of this game that received then lost the blessing of Matthias Cramer) because the theme of the original is so arbitrary and it looks kind of crummy. (Kind of like Castles of Burgundy, also from Ravensburger: a bunch of grim little tiles.) -- That said, the gameplay here is really smart, with some unusual quirks, and it plays super fast. The most interesting aspect is that the midgame penalizes you for falling behind in various categories (chieftains, special cards, whiskey)--but if you just grab a ton of stuff you'll be even more penalized at the end, because there's a stiff penalty for being greedy for tiles: minus 3 points per tile in excess of the SMALLEST fiefdom of the other players. (Thus if one player ends the game with 12 tiles and you have 18, you are penalized 6 x 3 = 18 points.) I appreciate games that make you play smart rather than greedy--if you grab a lot of stuff you have to actually make it EARN points to overcome the offset.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have played this game twice and I definitely like it. Beyond the fact that the game theme involves distilling whiskey and you gain victory points by activating a tavern, I really like the mechanic where you chose your next tile. I have been frustrated numerous times playing other tile games where I end up drawing tiles I can not use. There is a lot of tactics involved in deciding what tiles you can use and what tiles your opponent may want.

I also like the fact that you must plan ahead several moves so that you properly lay the tiles in the correct places in your tableau. Plus, you must move you meeples in the right spots. 

Gaining victory points based on differences in the number of whiskey barrels, special places and chieftains, is a unique twist and keeps people from focusing on one strategy to win the game.

A game very simple in rules but very deep in strategy and game-play.

RATING: N/A
I had heard a lot of positive things about this simple and compact game with its innovative action mechanisms. However, chally's excellent motivation for rating Glen More a 3 put me off completely.

(Quote from his review: "The idea of the game is that players move their meeple around a circular, tile-filled track, selecting tiles and adding them to their own individual tableau. Each time a new tile is added, that tile plus all surrounding tiles are activated (in the order of the player's choice). For those of you who have had the chance to play London, tile activation creates a fair bit of lonely downtime that is reminiscent of a player running a large city. The difference, of course, is that in Glen More this happens for every player on every turn of the game. A typical turn might involve (1) staring at the board weighing options, (2) selecting a tile, (3) staring at your tableau to decide where to put it, (4) placing the tile, (5) activating an adjacent tile that produces wheat, (6) activating an adjacent tile that produces cow, (7) activating an adjacent tile that produces stone, (8) using the newly placed building to change the wheat into a whisky barrel, (9) activating an adjacent building to convert the meat into VP, (10) selling the quarry to the market for 3 coins.")

Still, I'd like to see the action selection mechanism in... well, action.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
As the head of a Scottish clan in the 17th century, your turn consists of choosing a tile from the display and adding it your display. Your new tile has to go adjacent to a clan member, and you get to activate all adjacent tiles. If you race ahead in the display to grab tiles, other players can grab more tiles behind to catch up.

Tiles give resources, give VPs, or give you special abilities. There are three scoring rounds, when the tile deck in each round runs out. You then compare your whisky production, your chieftians and special cards to everyone else to score VPs. At the end game, you get penalized 3 VP per tile to the person with the lowest number of tiles. 

It's a fun game, with agonizing decisions on which tile to take and which tiles to activate. You don't have enough resources to do everything and always replayable with the order the tiles come out. I enjoy this game.

RATING: 8
Sleeper hit of 2011. Ok. So the cover is meh. The theme is meh. The illustrations are meh. The board, tiles, etc. are all meh. And I really hate green. But this game rocks. Really. This game has shown the designer, Matthias Cramer to be one to look out for in the coming years. He has since followed up with Lancaster (just got a copy) and Helvetia (highly rated @ Essen 2011). =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Decisions in Glen More are limited to choosing to take the next available tile or jump ahead to a tile you may want more and lose out on a turn or two, and where to place the newly acquired tile. Other decisions are essentially resource management.

The best feature of the game is its closed economy as prices of goods are based on player demand rather than an arbitrary figure.

With less than 4 players the game requires a dummy player represented by a die. Given how little actual player interaction there is in this game, one could conceivably play solo with 3 dummy dice and get a similar play experience. Unfortunately, with GM’s closed economy, the Dummy can remove certain resources from play early on that will cause shortages throughout the game.

I wonder if the Scots would be offended by the characterization of their heritage being reduced to sheep & cattle farming and whiskey production. Probably not, but if so I'm sure they'll just drink it off ;)

RATING: 8
This game was on my radar, although I was not too keen on it.  I am glad I played it now because there are some very unique mechanics in this game.  Most scoring is done by what you own compared to what your opponents own, so I have 1 whiskey, you have 5 whiskey, the difference is 4 and you look on the table to see how many points 4 is worth.  To acquire tiles whenever your meeple is the last in line you can move it to any tile you want, but be careful, because in the end if you have the most tiles you score -3 points for every tile more you have over the opponent with the least.  

The game felt very fresh, had lots of strategy, and played pretty quickly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Glen More is firmly rooted in the very familiar domain of tiles with special properties, wooden cubes, money, intricate scoring and a tagged-on topic: this time players progress along a track collecting tiles for their own display, laying them according to the positions of figures that move on top of them. Victory points can be increased by certain majorities at definite scoring rounds or by exploiting the properties of your display while the game evolves. The best results should be achieved with as few tiles as possible. The beautiful and slightly humorous setting is distilling whisky in Scotland.

Among these well-balanced and rather comfortable ingredients there is, though, one surprising mechanic that sets Glen More apart from the myriad of games in its genre - the player to move is always the last one on the track. This little kink makes the acquisition of tiles for your own display a more complicated affair, since players can have several moves in a row to develop their own displays; they might be penalised for the amount of tiles later on, but in turn that might not matter anymore considering the powerful machine they've helped build...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.9

Trovo l'astrattezza e l'aridità prevalere sul gioco e coprire il tentativo di ambientazione. E' vero che questo aspetto è presente in molti giochi ma Glen More è un titolo che ho rivenduto senza alcun pentimento e tutt'oggi non ho particolare piacere nel riprovarlo.

RATING: 8.5
I'm dabbling here somewhere between a game that could have lots of potential, and something that's just too random and unmanageable to be taken seriously. On the surface of things, there seems to be one too many VP-earning factor, and there's a routine 'going through the motions' to the gameplay that doesn't quite click. There can be an awful lot of downtime and fiddling with cubes, which sort of highlights the worst aspects of Euro-gaming.

On the other hand, the theme seems to make surprising sense, and the resource balancing will keep everyone involved until the final score tally. Doing sneaky stuff like locking up the market and hogging certain resources is very admirable.

Further Thoughts: Well, it was good enough for me to pimp my own version with some proper Resource-eeples and a dinky little storage tray. And it plays pretty swiftly with two or three. I guess it's a keeper now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The theme is primary production in Scotland, with a heavy emphasis on Whiskey. Excellent. Gameplay focuses on selection of new land areas (tiles) using a "you can't go back" mechanism similar to [i]Egizia[/i]. Tile placements follow rules similar to [i]Carcasssonne[/i] and generate production. Materials are processed by each players points-production-mechanism (their tile array) and generate victory points or improvements that score big at the end. It's a Euro!
And it is a very, very good Euro too. As is often the case, the principal player interaction comes through blocking moves- but in Glen More these are easy to find and often quite vicious. Turns involve a lot of decisions (what tile to take, where to place it, how to use the resources) but since these choices often impact other players, everyone will be attentive and interested. The game clicks along at a good pace.

RATING: 8
[b]Glen More[/b]

COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8 -> 8)[/COLOR]

[i]I've been lucky enough to play this a few more times even though I don't own a copy. After three plays I've seen multiple strategies lead to victory as long as you focus your efforts. Glen More is definitely not a point salad game, if you try and do a little of everything you won't do well. The special tiles add another opportunity to find ways to get even more out of the engine you've built. Of course all of this is easier said than done because of the tile placement/activation rules surrounding the game. Glen More is one of those games that manages to achieve the perfect balance
  
Last year I thought Glen More was getting a reprint, but in fact it is actually going to be a 2.0 version that appears to still be a ways off. For now I am content to play my buddy's copy any time it hits the table and wait and see what Cramer has in store for the second version of Glen More when it's released.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I was very excited to finally play Glen More, it's been on my radar since I got into the hobby almost four years ago. However; it went out of print before I decided to get a copy so it took a while to finally get the opportunity to play. 

Now that I've played both Glen More and Kraftwagon I definitely prefer the game play of Glen More. I compare both games because they share a similar mechanic when it comes to determining player order. There is basically a rondel of sorts and you can move forward as far as you want along the track, but it won't be your turn again until everyone else's markers are ahead of yours. This makes for some agonizing choices in Glen More because there are times when you really want to jump ahead and not worry about how many possible tiles the players behind you will get. Also Glen More will penalize you if you just take every tile others have passed since you can lose a lot of points depending on how many more tiles you have compared to the person with the least. I felt like this balancing mechanism was missing in Kraftwagon and it almost always made sense for the other player to take every action the previous player skipped over (which is especially true in the 2p game). 

Besides that I just found the decisions you make in Glen More to be more interesting. The combination of tile laying with the need to have a worker on an adjacent tile to place it makes for a strategic game where you need to plan ahead so you don't create a tableau of tiles where it is no longer possible to activate the tiles you need to. In the end I'm very glad D=Glen More is getting a second edition and I'll be picking up a copy once it becomes available.[/i]

RATING: 6
A tile collection game a la Vikings where the tiles you choose are placed carefully into your display, but it has layers of complexity wrought over and around it to produce a more thoughtful (but less fun) beast. Choose any tile on the track you like - some will be free, others will require cubes to purchase. Place it into your display next to one of your meeples (which eventually need to move around to provide you future places to build), and then trigger not only the tile you score, but every tile around it as well. Tile actions may be to generate cubes (ie the currencies), trade cubes in for vp's, get/move a meeple, and so on. Each turn is a consideration of placement for now (what's everything that will trigger), and to plan for future turn triggerings (what am I aiming to collect in future to take advantage of this placement now). That leads into your master VP earning strategy and more specifically what VP races you want to be part of and which to ignore (most meeples, most whisky, etc) which will score three times throughout. You'll only get 7 to 12 turns say (depending on how far you leap ahead to claim tiles on the display), so each turn is important and must complement previous and future turns. All good stuff. But there's so much to consider, including determining the likelihood of the tile you want next being left by the others, that the downtime can be a real turnoff, especially if you've down a leapahead and you're waiting for the others to work through multiple turns until they all pass you again. With 4 players we found it too slow, so will restrict to less in future. A very tight game, well produced, but be prepared for a slow game, and this cruelled it for me in the end.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5

You must try and achieve majority in several goods and other items, while using as few buildings as possible. RATING: 6
A fine little euro.  This is a sweet game that plays in an hour.  Its well put together and there is a lot going on.  The tile draft is the best part, but I also love the tile placement, scoring, and multiple small VP engines.  Each of the above listed aspects is innovative in some small way, and the game feels fresh.  It has a simple, closed free-market economy that is robust enough that the game doesn't break even if there is an unexpected surfeit or shortage of a commodity.

Strategically, the key is to compete in at least one, or better yet two, of the three scoring categories (hats, barrels, real estate), and ALSO have at least one brown-tile VP engine going. But it is very possible to win by specializing.

Its thematic in the euro tradition.  I think its lovely.  You can make some scotch!  Its far more thematic than Goa, which might be Glen More's closest comparison.  It main thematic fault is that in the game you need wheat to make scotch.  That's wrong: scotch is made from barley!!  

Glen More scales well from two to five although three and four are the best counts.  The dummy player, for 2 and 3 handed games, is very simply integrated into the game–it actually makes the game better rather than worse. 

The rulebook could have been larger. The type is small. In fact the whole game could have been printed a wee bit larger. I normally hate over-boxed and over-produced games, but this one deserves a little more cardboard.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
There are a lot of interesting concepts in this tile-laying game.  The tile selection felt like a combination of Egizia and Thebes--you can't move backwards, and the person furthest behind in the queue always goes next, potentially getting more tiles.  Tiles activating upon placement really helps streamline the turns.  Getting more tiles can be good and bad--you get more activations and potentially more points, but the game penalizes you at the end for number of tiles, so you need to make sure they're at least worth that much.  I liked this a lot, and look forward to playing more.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 7
This is a solid game, where players walk their meeple in a circle taking tiles, which they add to their board based on certain placement rules, then they activate the ability of the tile they took and all tiles surrounding it. The player who is standing at the back of the line is the active player, so at times players might jump ahead for a strong tile, but then lose out on a turn. The scoring is based on comparing your scoring resource to the person who has the least of that resource, so it's good to specialize if you can. Lots of fun, very intelligent game, however this game does suffer from component problems, iconography unclarity and a few unclear special buildings.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 10
I tend to enjoy tile-building games whether it is on a shared board or on individual tableaus. I enjoy determining how far to jump and the tiles to get, managing your area size and making sure you're not going to be penalized for additional tiles over the smallest player, and timing things to take advantage of the three scoring rounds. For me the defining element of this game is the quick play as we can usually play in about 30-45 minutes and I get a lot of the satisfaction I get in longer games like Ora et Labora.

RATING: N/A
I don't think I "get" optimization games like Glen More and Dominion. I can understand them and win them, but they're not fun. Or maybe they're just not fun for two players.

Glen More failed for us for the same reasons the base Dominion did, and in fact they feel like the same game. You pick a strategy early on (butcher, whisky, variety (fairs), special tiles, etc) and then work through the mechanics of the game until you reach an arbitrary end condition (last tile has been placed) to see if your strategy was more optimal than that of your opponents. Aside from taking a tile the other person wanted or clogging up the market in one way or another, there's almost no direct conflict or interaction between players. I can watch what you're doing and react to it, but mostly I'm just going to do my thing while you do yours and hope mine works better.

I think we prefer games with a victory goal (Battleline = three flags in a row; Hive = surround the queen; Catan = first to 10; Attika = first to make a path or place all tiles; Barons = first to build a cathedral; etc) or at least where the score is more open and obvious as you go along (Bohnanza; Carcassone without farmers).

Going to give it another shot before passing judgement. I want to be wrong about this game and games like it. I want to learn to like these games.

UPDATE: The store where we bought this has a fantastic return policy. We took it back. I think the game was mechanically excellent and if we liked games /like/ this, we'd probably very much like this game. That said, it just wasn't for us and I'm not going to give it a rating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
June 2017: Downgrade fro 6 to 3

Too many rules for what the game is. I'm losing patience with these kinds of games. As with so many Euros, it's really about playing the system and much less about playing against other players.

At least it still plays relatively quickly.

March 2016: A pleasant surprise (as I'm not a fan of Euros in general).

There are a lot of mechanisms in this game, and they work quite tightly together: turn order dictated by which tile you drew, restrictive tile laying, supply-and-demand mechanism, and lots and lots of special powers. Surprisingly smooth play! Still, the tile laying rules I find a bit too overly complicated.

But like many Euros, the game rewards players who focus on their own strategies rather than getting in the way of others. 

The Thebes-like mechanism of determining turn order is strange here; you're rewarded more for taking less turns than more. So if there's a tile you really want, you go ahead and get it; you don't wait to hopefully get extra turns.

RATING: 5
Played 2x but so far it is very fun. There is some strategy and some tactics and some interesting tableu building. Time will tell how well this lasts. 

-May 2012

I really didn't dig Glen More. It has this mechanic I found annoying in which you move your pawn forward along the tile market. The pawn that is furthest back goes first and can either skip ahead past some or all opponent pawns or or move to and purchase a tile that is behind some or all opponents. Thus impacting how many tiles they will get compared to opponents and impacting turn order. 

It was always hard to decide whether to move far past opponents to lock down a highly desired tile or to wait and hope they don't hate draft it from you. I don't enjoy hate drafting or being inefficient and this game made you risk one or the other. :(

Many people seem to like the tension that decision requires. Not me... 
~7/20/15

RATING: 8.5
Tile collection, placement, and activation for the production of goods, which can be collected for victory points, sold for money, etc. The innovation in this game is the unique turn order mechanic (seemingly inspired by Peter Prinz's Thebes, and with some similarities to that in Tikal II), which allows the player "furthest back" in the chain of tiles to continue to take turns until they are no longer last. This may encourage taking less optimal tiles in order to get another turn; however each selected tile must be placed in a legal (and sometimes optimal, because it activates all tiles around it) open space in the player's display, removing that space for a future tile. Alea Medium Box game family.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 8.98
this game was awesome.  A pure euro at heart however with a thin theme but we were happy there was a theme their rather than it be purely abstract.  This is kind of, in my mind, a cross between Alhambra, Thebes, and with resource management thrown in.  Great w/ two and a real brain burner.  You build city-type area via tile placement rules, you get resources based on various parameters and you use these resources to attempt to better your opponents victory points in various parameters. however, as in Thebes the more aggressive you are at getting tiles the more opportunity you give to you opponents because they may get more turns than you.  A really brain burner.  Pretty easy rules but superb depth with the game.  After three plays, this really is a tactical strategy game.  You need to determine a strategy a some point but it's needs to be done sometime in the middle round.  Incredible game that seems to play differently each time.

This means that different players will be working on different goals.  But one needs to be efficient as the number of tiles in one's tableau is also a scoring unit. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
An Alea small box game that deserves the big box treatment. The components are serviceable, but thicker tiles would have been nice.

The novel turn-order mechanic is a pretty interesting take on the Thebes time track. It creates some tough decisions based on assessing the desirability of a tile, and how many turns is it worth giving up in exchange. The warehouse is fascinating, and it adds a few, minor economic considerations each turn in addition to added flexibility for tile purchases.

The area of each player will grow as tiles are added each turn. Placement alone provides for some interesting strategic play. There's no prescribed way for the land to grow, which allows for some creative tile actions.

Out of all the "no direct conflict" games I've played, Glen More seems to have the most player interaction out of them all. Each player has to keep track of scoring majorities and the current state of the warehouse.

Open ended with plenty of paths to victory, a nice little game that deserves to be the crown gem of the small box line.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Even as an AT'er, I LOVE THIS GAME. :laugh:

Sure, there's no plundering or fighting (or farms!), but drinking (distilleries), more drinking (taverns), slaughtering (butchers) and partying (annual faires) are part of the game. One of your men even "mysteriously disappears" (is paid as a cost) when you place the Loch Ness tile! The mechanics of the game are more intricate than some of the simpler Eurogames, yet you're not blindsided by some annoying arbitrary themeless mechanic.

As a newbie bonus, you can play the first round (each game has three rounds) as a 20-minute "practice round". All information is public, so it's easy to help other players.

BTW, We call the chieftans middle managers. They're worth points but don't actually do anything once they're promoted!

RATING: 6
It's a lovely game of trying to create an efficient point making machine through tile selection. What elevates this variant of that mechanic is the somewhat inelegant, yet interesting tile placement system. You're trying to activate certain previously bought tiles by placing new tiles next to them. That's more difficult and challenging than it sounds. Another nice element to the game is the scoring mechanic. You're scoring points in three categories against the player with the fewest in that category. If you can engineer a nice jump on one opponent early in the game there's some points to rake in, but at what cost to your nicely balanced and planned village? Make points against the others, but find that they make them back in another category. It's thought provoking.

This scales well from 2-5 players, but plays differently. I was concerned with five players the control would be lost, but there's still plenty of tiles to choose from on your turn. The game comes in under an hour once you get the system of moving clan members around, what kinds of tiles are out there, how to get a production engine going, etc. There's a few different paths to victory with the markets and special tiles.

Downsides:
1. The 2 & 3 player games have a dummy player pinching random tiles. Some people might not like that.
2. Player interaction is lowish. Sometimes you'll take a tile because it's just too good to leave for another player. You'll cast an eye on what the others are up to and how they're scoring. On the whole though you're just playing your own game.

It's a very likeable game that will stand up to repeated playings. Good job Alea - this is perfect for your mid-box range - low to medium difficulty, playable in an hour, but a reasonably meaty offering (like Louis XIV). Well worth the price and doesn't hog the shelf space.

Upon further play I can't help but feel that the game would be a better one if there was not the three point penalty for each tile more than the person with the lowest number of tiles. Firstly, it would reduce some of the downtime players experience who go charging off to the front each turn. Secondly, it would make the decision of choosing your tiles just a bit harder - charging off to collect a good tile further ahead would no longer be the obvious move if there were a couple of semi-decent tiles along the way.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Played three times over the course of 5 years. Glen More hits a number of right notes for me for being a relatively small and quick game packed with tricky decisions. Its turn order track is the primary attraction – there is so much angst in jumping ahead to nab a good tile, and enduring the painful wait for your turn to roll around. But even those who meander behind have to watch out for the penalty that bites back for having too many tiles. Hah!

The other point of interest is the scoring system which gauges how well players are doing in accumulating particular elements (special cards, chieftains and whiskey barrels) in relation to other players. Depending on your position in the game, you'd sometimes want to close a scoring gap, and at other times widen another. The other considerations, such as the spatial element in triggering the effects of tiles and the mini-market for resources, fit nicely into the framework.

The only thing I wish for is that the size of the tiles and typeface be increased for legibility's sake. The gameplay is also admittedly a little dry and calculative at times, but otherwise Glen More is upper-tier material amongst the Alea medium box titles.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nice little Euro where you draft tiles with the Neuland/Jenseit/Tutankhamen system.  You create a little map with them based on landscape and the positioning of your workers.  Then you creatively produce resources by building tiles by previous tiles. 

Cute, with some worthwhile ways to try varying the way you build your "empire".  Certainly, a multiple paths to victory game.  There are three checks that pit your progress against other players, so it isn't a completely solitaire experience, but really the other players could be reproduced by a randomly generated number device (like, say, the die that does this exact thing in the two/three player game).

If you like converting cubes into victory points then this is a worthwhile place to do it.  If you are looking for more interaction other than losing out on drafting a tile now and again or comparing the size of your Parliament, then look elsewhere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
This game has much more depth than most other "tile placement" games.  In fact, it plays much more like a boardgame.

The game is packed with tense tactical decisions such as which tile to select (and how that impacts others and your future turns), how to develop your village, where you wish your black meeple to be in your village and when you should start collecting chieftans.  It is short and quick and always leaves me wanting to play again ... 

I highly recommend this game.

RATING: 10
LOVE THIS TO DEATH!

It is such an amazing game. My only complaint is that i dont like to play this at higher play counts with really slow players. It can get really long at that point, and you may feel like you don't have much interaction at all with some of the other players. It is a very good two or three player game - really excellent with two. I would even play with 4, but never again with 5! 

The geometric element of placing your tiles in this game is great because it is very simple, and doesn't feel like it's a huge part of the game. Much of the game is on the shared board, as it is an excruciating choice sometimes whether to take a tile that you need, or to take a chance of someone else taking it. This choice is so great because of the very elegant mechanic that often you can take the tile you want, but that will mean that you wont get another turn for a while - brilliant! 

Love the theme, gameplay, and bits - all awesome in a little package too!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1 play

This is a nice combination of tile laying (Alhambra style: build your own little area) with tile selection (sort of like Egizia: in one direction only) and turn order (like Neuland and Thebes: last player goes next).

Here, you're a Scottish clansman collecting tiles (they produce goods, give you more clansmen to work with, etc.) by moving your token forward along the path of tiles. Tile placement is also clever: you can place them adjacent to your meeple (assuming it fits otherwise). Placing a tile activates it and all 8 adjacent tiles -- cool! Tiles are not totally random; instead, they are grouped into 3 groups, and scoring happens after going through each stack. You score for a variety of things (e.g., number of whisky tokens, # of chieftains, etc.). In addition, at the end of the game you lose points for having collected too many tiles. Most points at the end wins. The player with the most points wins.

The game played pretty quickly and was full of little decisons along the way. Like Egizia, the simple choice of when to jump ahead and when to hang back provides plenty of tension. The tile-laying part is not terribly difficult -- you can find a spot for most tiles -- but you do have to plan for it by placing/moving your meeples appropriately. 

Good game, looking forward to playing it more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Not truly elegant, the tile picking mechanic doesn't really balance the random nature of the tiles, but it helps a lot, making this much better than a card game with tiles might be.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a tile-laying game where each player takes turns selecting a tile from a track and placing it in their own personal tableau.  Each tile has a different ability and placing a tile activates its ability as well as all surrounding tile abilities.  These abilities range from collecting resources (which are used to purchase tiles as well as some ability activations) to collecting points.  The point scoring is reasonably interesting where each player scores points based on the difference between their count of certain items and the player with the lowest count in that particular item.  But otherwise this is a reasonably run-of-the-mill tile-laying game.  Except for the turn order mechanism.

The player whose piece is furthest toward the back of the tile auction track always gets to go first.  That player can take any tile they like on the track whether it is simply one space ahead of their piece or all the way around the track.  This means the turn order is very variable and players can take multiple turns in a row or go several turns without getting one of their own if they jumped way ahead for a specific tile.

The one minor thing I think is pretty neat is because of this variable number and order of turns one player could potentially end up with many more tiles and thus ability activations than others.  The final scoring somewhat accommodates for this because each tile a player has over the player with the least LOSES them 3 points at the end of the game.

That turn order mechanic is really what elevates an otherwise pretty standard, dry, and honestly not that exciting tile-laying game.  The artwork is drab and the components aren't the best either.  But I really do enjoy that turn order mechanic enough to enjoy the game overall and give it a recommendation.

RATING: 10
Reasons I like this game:
-Good integration of theme and mechanics.
-Simple and effective market system allows exchange of goods and points.
-Interesting decisions each turn, and very tense downtime.
-Players are rewarded for planning ahead, but the game requires tactical finesse as well.
-There are multiple strategies that can win the game; learning them is fun and rewarding.
-The game can be played casually and competitively.
-The order the tiles appear is different each time; the game has high replay value.

Reasons I hate this game:
-The iconography on the tiles is rather small.
-Its not really a family game so I don't get to play very often.

TL;DR: One of the greatest euro-games in the genre. Highly replayable, deep, tense, strategic, tactical...In fact I could go for a game right now...

RATING: 7.5
Not quite sure yet how much chaos rules this one, even with 3.  There are numerous paths to victory, but it is easily possible for your plans to be foiled by other players, or the die if playing 2 or 3.  The random factor of when tiles come out, combined with the the chaos added by your opponents might make it a little too tactical, but, on the other hand it plays fairly quickly so perhaps in balance it is good. *****

After more play we find that this game works great for two players--and the ghost player is an often nerve-wracking push-your-element, which truly adds to the game, rather than just "making it work."  Two or three players seems pretty optimal to us, as more players increases the player-driven chaos, and pushes the game progressively more towards tactical at the expense of the more decision-based strategy found at 2 and 3.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A nice, compact victory point engine game. Basically a game for 4, a "robot" player is added when playing with 3. The players leap-frog around a (topologically) circular track which is kept filled with various tiles which grant resources (men and material) and special powers. There are an initial set of tiles, 0 (zero), and then 1 to 3, which denote phases of the game. Victory points are awarded for completing certain actions, and for having more whiskey, parliament votes, and special tiles at the end of each phase.

Two interesting factors hold greed in check, If you really want a tile, you have to leap far ahead to make sure you get it, since your opponents can leap ahead, too. This means you aren't going to get your next turn for some time--until they've all passed you. On the other hand, at the very end of the game, you get assessed 3 points per tile, and it's difficult to get tiles to produce that many VPs in the game. Yet when it's your turn to leap frog, you either take the best tile available to you, or grab tiles you don't really need (costing -3 VP at the end of the game) trying to get the tile you really want.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
For a card-drafting, engine-building game, I like this one. The player interaction is low, but competing for tiles and income via the warehouse adds enough to keep things interesting. I also enjoy the rule that penalizes players for building a large village. While counter-intuitive at first, this penalty provides yet another inter-player vector to monitor (in addition to the chieftains, cards, and whiskey).

Ultimately, it's the fact that players must not only build a victory point engine, but also do so in step with the other players that makes this game a winner in a genre that I normally write off as nothing more than a multi-player solitaire exercise in optimization. Glen More does not save the genre for me, and it won't be my favorite game, but it does have its charms, and I can bring it out with players who don't share my passion for high interaction contests.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Outstanding tile-laying game that incorporates many familiar mechanics (rondel-like variable turn order, spatial decision-making, resource timing/management) into an easily digestible whole.  

The only knock I can give this game is that with low player counts (2 or 3), there is not really that much competition for resources and thus the market is seldom used.  I suspect the tension of acquiring the correct resources would ratchet up with 4 or 5 players.  

So long, Carcassonne!

5 player game was much tougher.

RATING: 7.75
Glen More was neat to play as we just returned from the Scottish Highlands.

The game itself has some fun, unique qualities. Movement is similar to Patchwork, but you can choose to claim any tile on the board. However, the further you move along the track the more opportunity opponents have to claim multiple (albeit less powerful) tiles.

This is offset a bit by the final scoring, where any player who has more tiles than the player with the least number of tiles is penalized significant points.

Hopefully this gets a reprint soon.

RATING: 8
Glen More is a game for gamers. It has some new mechanisms. The mechanism for selection of tiles is nice (not great because most of the time it feels like players are getting the tiles in turn order, but it improves as the game comes to its end (players leapfrog to get better tiles). The mechanism for the market is great (more games should adopt this). The mechanism for scoring is based on comparison of your progress vs the other players which very balancing and nice (I wish they had a scoring track around the board instead of victory little tiles). The mechanism of placing and activating tiles is brilliant and fresh. The problem with the game is that the tiles should have been made larger and with better standard illustrations of what they produce and how they function. Also more cubes are necessary (they should have added 3 extra cubes in each color). It is a tricky game that it would have been a great game if only the tiles had a better and more consistent description on them of how they work. As you can tell from my comments, a great game but whoever was head of the art department clearly downgraded the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a tile laying game where you pick tiles from a circular track.  You don't get to play again until the other players catch up to you.  So you can jump ahead to grab something you really need, but you won't move again for a while.  Which might not be a bad idea in some cases as having more tiles in your tableau than other players counts against you at game end.  

There are three scoring rounds where you're judged on how much whiskey you have, how many chieftains, and how many special sites you've built.  The higher you are than the worst player the better, up to a point where it's capped.

When you lay a tile it activates and triggers all it's neighbors.  Tiles have some placement restrictions... tiles must match up to either your one river (north-south) or your one road (east-west) or a tile with neither.  Additionally you can only place a tile adjacent to a Chieftain which can make moving Chieftains an important decision.

There are a bunch of tiles, and you're going to have to keep checking what the special tiles do.  The tile iconography isn't sufficient for them.

Overall it's a clever little game with interesting decisions that plays fairly quickly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[thing=66362][/thing] is a strange game at first. The turn order caused some frowning in my friends, that struggled a little with it. Also, the notion that when you place your newly acquired place, all the location adjacent (even diagonally) are activated - this also caused confusion throughout the whole game, especially because of the diagonally thing and the need of a clan member near by. Some of the players got all this by the third turn, more or less, others were doing things wrong all the way to the end.

Also, the buying and selling of goods worked until a point: after all the stones and wood were bought, no one else wanted to sell them - the ones that have them, didn't wanted to "give" the chance for the others to purchase such goods, trying to hamper the tile-acquiring abilities of the other players. So the market was basically "locked" from early turns until the very end (when someone then sell goods to get the coins, to use them for VPs). Maybe this is usual, maybe I was playing with bastards :), by now I can't say.

Even with this perks, I liked [thing=66362][/thing] quite a bit, and basically all the other players in the table also. It is a smart game, that went on fast and gives a lot to think about (from the choice of a tile, to its placing, its uses, and its relation to what the others have). All of this in the 1 hour time.

I need to explore this game further, so its rating will probably change in the near future.

********

The rate did change: up! From 7.5 now to a solid 8.

RATING: 6
My first actual time track game. I don't think it's very good, but +.5 for coming in a small box and not taking too long to play.

The point of the time track is supposed to be that you can sacrifice turns in order to leap ahead to grab something of high value. I see a couple of things that keep this from being interesting in Glen More. First, especially early in the game I don't feel like the values among various tiles vary that much. Secondly, you won't even be able to take a number of tiles because of placement restrictions. (This also limits the potential for hate drafting.)

The game penalizes players at game end who have taken more tiles than others. I guess this is supposed to give players a push in the direction of jumping further down the track, even if there is nothing so much more valuable there.

The chieftain placement system is a little displeasing. It governs where you can place new tiles and which tiles you will be able to use, but the decisions don't seem difficult.

I like the supply/demand-based market system for the various goods, but I didn't usually feel I had enough control to make it part of a strategy. The game's shortness doesn't help here.

The short and chaotic feel also makes the game feel more like solitaire.

See chally's comments with his 3 rating.

RATING: 5
The standout mechanic of Glen More is the tile drafting mechanism, which implements the time concept from games like Neuland, Thebes and Tinner's Trail. There is also a mild spatial element to deal with as you place your acquired tiles in your personal tableau, not entirely unlike Alhambra or Vikings. 

In our recent four player game I was largely absent minded while my opponents debated where to place their tiles. I don't think the issue is so much that there's a long time between turns so much as there's so little to think about. I felt like once I committed to a strategy early on the choice of which tile to take was mostly obvious once it came around to my turn. I dislike when in games I decide what I'm going to do and then spend all this time executing the decision I already made. This is why the game Lost Valley doesn't work for me: you have to walk to point A to get a resource and then come back to your base and then walk to point B and then come back to your base and then go... all to just do a simple conversion that you decided on 20 minutes ago. Glen More isn't quite as onerous but in a real sense it's like the whole game is one protracted execution of a decision you made early on without anything of interest along the way.

►COMMENTAIRE(S) ABRÉGÉ(S):
± THÈME : plutôt plaqué; mais agrémente le jeu.
± Aide de jeu : 2 inclus mais j’aurais aimé qu’il y en ait 1 par joueur. Manque certaines précisions importantes (j’aurais apprécié un tableau résumant les pouvoirs des différents châteaux classés selon leur pile d’appartenance).
+ Configurations équilibrées selon le nombre de joueurs.
+ Icônes : sont faciles à comprendre ou à mémoriser.

►REMARQUE(S) PERSONNELLE(S):
® Je recommande son essai car il pourrait être apprécié par d’autres joueurs.

1e essai à 4 joueurs. À première vue, il avait piqué ma curiosité parce qu’il m'avait rappelé Carcassonne (pose de tuiles) tout en possédant des mécaniques assez différentes pour soulever mon intérêt lors de la lecture des règles. Ce fût donc une déception pour moi lorsque notre partie-découverte s'est révélée plutôt lourde (ennui généralisé). Entre autres, je n’ai pas apprécié les règles du chemin et de la rivière uniques qui m’ont semblé plus irritantes que pertinentes. Cependant, d'autres mécaniques du jeu m'ont semblé intéressantes, tel le circuit des tuiles et le marché, mais pas assez pour contrebalancer mon ressenti global négatif.

RATING: 7
This is the game that put Matthias Cramer on the map and for good reason. Mixing a Carcassonne style tile laying game with a fairly complex resource management game, Glen More hits all the notes that pure Euro fans love. The real trick however, is that Cramer manages to strip out every speck of unnecessary fat. This game can be taught in 10 minutes, but feels much more complex, as building your tableau of tiles for peak efficiency is a real brain burn.

It's crazy to me that this has stayed out of print for so long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I might just trick this game out - it's that good!

RATING: 9
It's got the feel of most resource mgmt titles without the ungainly stash of card and wood. There are three reasons why this is enjoyable: 

1: The flexible queue-like turn order system which allows bonkers leaps ahead to pick exactly what you want at the cost of waiting out a few turns, or a slow creep around the tile board soaking up everything in sight while everyone has to wait for you to catch up.

2: The satisfying triggering of neighbouring tiles when placing a new tile. 

3: The seperate puzzle of clansmen movement which governs your web-like sprawl across the table

RATING: 7.5
Increased my rating of Glen More now that I've (finally) played it with two. Thought it would never see the table again due to competition at four-five players. The dummy (dice) 'player' works fine in this game; you almost don't notice its presence.

Glen More is a very abstract game of getting stuff to buy more stuff to gain victory points, but it has a neat turn order mechanic where you can decide to jump ahead to gain a tile you really want but then have to wait until the pack catches up to you before you can go again.

Good game, a bit economic for me but unique enough to keep playing.

RATING: 7.7
O my god, what is the text on the tiles small. So, now I have complained about it, and that’s it. I mean, I can keep talking about it, but you have to deal with it while you’re playing.

While playing, you’ll notice that this is a easy flowing game. The way the turn-order is determined is different then usual, and it makes it possible that if your opponent makes a big move forwards, that you might be able to take several turns after each-other. But you’ll have to keep in mind that for every tile that you own more then your opponents at the end of the game, will cost you three points. A way to avoid that loss, is to make sure every tile you take will give you three points minimum. The game mechanics are not very original. The way the turn-order is determined, we’ve seen before (for example in Neuland). But in this game, it just flows very well. Very pleasant to play. Not a game for families.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Combines Irondale, Alhambra, and Shipyard features.

Move around a Shipyard-style tile circle to pick up tiles.  Play them to your realm Alhambra-style.  Trigger played and adjacent tiles, Irondale-style, to get and use resources.  

I liked the movement mechanic a lot: you can jump ahead on the circle to acquire an especially tasty tile, but since the player at the back always gets the next turn, it might be a while before you get to go again.

Scoring is triggered three times as tile stacks are diminished.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.2
Quite a lot of game into such a small box. This game somehow encourages you to think through and weight your every move, as impact on scoring is public and involves every player, resulting in long and boring games with AP players. That's the wrong way of playing it though, as after few plays it's evident that the tile draw scrambles most deterministic evaluations.
Played smoothly and without weighting too much every action this is an extremely enjoyable and deep enough experience that blends together many mechanics in a clever way. Probably works best with 3-4 players.
